<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 39" name="FNopinion1.39" href="#FRopinion1.39">39</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;<em>Justice Kennedy</em> accuses us of engaging in a sleight of hand by conflating "unseemly corporate speech" with the speech of political parties and candidates, and then adverting to the "corporate speech rationale as if it were the linchpin of the litigation."  <em>Post</em>, at 7 (opinion concurring in part and dissenting in part).  This is incorrect.  The principles set forth here and relied upon in assessing Title I are the same principles articulated in <em>Buckley</em> and its progeny that regulations of contributions to candidates, parties, and political committees are subject to less rigorous scrutiny than direct restraints on speech--including "unseemly corporate speech."</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 40" name="FNopinion1.40" href="#FRopinion1.40">40</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Since our decision in <em>Buckley</em>, we have consistently applied less rigorous scrutiny to contribution restrictions aimed at the prevention of corruption and the appearance of corruption.  See, <em>e.g.</em>, 453 U.&nbsp;S. 182, 195-196 (1981) (plurality opinion) (applying less rigorous scrutiny to FECA's $5,000 limit on contributions to multicandidate political committees);  <em>National Right to Work</em>, 533 U.&nbsp;S. 431, 456 (2001) (applying less rigorous scrutiny to expenditures coordinated with a candidate); <em>Federal Election Comm'n </em>v<em>. Beaumont, </em>539 U.&nbsp;S.___, ___ (2003) (slip op., at 14-15) (applying less rigorous scrutiny to provisions intended to prevent circumvention of otherwise valid contribution limits).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 41" name="FNopinion1.41" href="#FRopinion1.41">41</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Indeed, Congress structured 323(b) in such a way as to free individual, corporate, and union donations to state committees for <em>nonfederal </em>elections from federal source and amount restrictions.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 42" name="FNopinion1.42" href="#FRopinion1.42">42</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;<em>Justice Kennedy</em>'s contention that less rigorous scrutiny applies only to regulations burdening political association, rather than political speech, misreads <em>Buckley</em>.  In <em>Buckley</em>, we recognized that contribution limits burden both protected speech and association, though they generally have more significant impacts on the latter.  528 U.&nbsp;S. 377, 388 (2000) ("While we did not [in <em>Buckley</em>] attempt to parse [the] distinctions between the speech and association standards of scrutiny for contribution limits, we did make it clear that those restrictions bore more heavily on the associational right than on [the] freedom to speak.  We consequently proceeded on the understanding that a contribution limitation surviving a claim of associational abridgment would survive a speech challenge as well, and we held the standard satisfied by the contribution limits under review." (citation omitted)).  It is thus simply untrue in the campaign finance context that all "burdens on speech necessitate strict scrutiny review."  <em>Post</em>, at 29. </p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 43" name="FNopinion1.43" href="#FRopinion1.43">43</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;<em>Justice Kennedy</em> is no doubt correct that the associational burdens imposed by a particular piece of campaign-finance regulation may at times be so severe as to warrant strict scrutiny.  <em>Ibid.  </em>In light of our interpretation of 323(a), however, see <em>infra, </em>at 46-47, 323 does not present such a case.  As <em>Justice Kennedy</em> himself acknowledges, even "<em>significant</em> interference" with "protected rights of association" are subject to less rigorous scrutiny.  <em>Beaumont</em>, 539 U.&nbsp;S., at _____ (slip op., at 15); see <em>post</em>, at 28.  There is thus nothing inconsistent in our decision to account for the particular associational burdens imposed by 323(a) when applying the appropriate level of scrutiny. </p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 44" name="FNopinion1.44" href="#FRopinion1.44">44</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The fact that the post-1990 explosion in soft-money spending on federal electioneering was accompanied by a series of efforts in Congress to clamp down on such uses of soft money (culminating, of course, in BCRA) underscores the fact that the FEC regulations permitted more than Congress, in enacting FECA, had ever intended.  See J. Cantor, Congressional Research Service Report for Congress: Campaign Finance Legislation in the 101st Congress (1990) (9 bills seeking to limit the influence of soft money introduced); J. Cantor, CRS Report for Congress: Campaign Finance Legislation in the 102nd Congress (1991) (10 such bills introduced); J. Cantor, CRS Report for Congress: Campaign Finance Legislation in the 103rd Congress (1993) (16 bills); J. Cantor, CRS Report for Congress: Campaign Finance Legislation in the 104th Congress (1996) (18 bills); see also 251 F.&nbsp;Supp. 2d, at 201-206 <em>(per curiam)</em> (discussing legislative efforts to curb soft money in 105th and subsequent Congresses).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 45" name="FNopinion1.45" href="#FRopinion1.45">45</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;<em>Justice Kennedy</em> contends that the plurality's observation in <em>Colorado I</em> that large soft-money donations to a political party pose little threat of corruption "establish[es] that" such contributions are not corrupting.  <em>Post</em>, at 17-18 (citing <em>Colorado I</em>, 518 U.&nbsp;S. 604, 616, 617-618 (1996)).  The cited dictum has no bearing on the present case.  <em>Colorado I </em>addressed an entirely different question--namely, whether Congress could permissibly limit a party's independent expenditures--and did so on an entirely different set of facts.  It also had before it an evidentiary record frozen in 1990--well before the soft-money explosion of the 1990's.  See <em>Federal Election Comm'n</em> v. <em>Colorado Republican Fed. Campaign Comm.</em>, 839 F.&nbsp;Supp. 1448, 1451 (Colo. 1993).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 46" name="FNopinion1.46" href="#FRopinion1.46">46</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Other business leaders agreed.  For example, the chairman of the board and CEO of a major toy company explained:</p>
<p>

"&nbsp;'Many in the corporate world view large soft money donations as a cost of doing business. ... I remain convinced that in some of the more publicized cases, federal officeholders actually appear to have sold themselves and the party cheaply.  They could have gotten even more money, because of the potential importance of their decisions to the affected business.'&nbsp;"  251 F.&nbsp;Supp. 2d, at 491 (Kollar-Kotelly, J.) (quoting declaration of Alan G. Hassenfeld, CEO, Hasbro, Inc.,   ;16; see 6-R Defs. Exhs., Tab 17).</p>
<p>

Similarly the chairman emeritus of a major airline opined:</p>
<p>

"&nbsp;'Though a soft money check might be made out to a political party, labor and business leaders know that those checks open the doors of the offices of individual and important Members of Congress and the Administration...&nbsp;. Labor and business leaders believe--based on experience and with good reason--that such access gives them an opportunity to shape and affect governmental decisions and that their ability to do so derives from the fact that they have given large sums of money to the parties.'&nbsp;"  251 F.&nbsp;Supp. 2d, at 498 (Kollar-Kotelly, J.) (quoting Greenwald Decl.   ;12, App. 283-284,   ;10); 251 F.&nbsp;Supp. 2d, at 858-859 (Leon, J.) (same).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 47" name="FNopinion1.47" href="#FRopinion1.47">47</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Even more troubling is evidence in the record showing that national parties have actively exploited the belief that contributions purchase influence or protection to pressure donors into making contributions.  As one CEO explained:</p>
<p>

"&nbsp;'[I]f you're giving a lot of soft money to one side, the other side knows.  For many economically-oriented donors, there is a risk in giving to only one side, because the other side may read through FEC reports and have staff or a friendly lobbyist call and indicate that someone with interests before a certain committee has had their contributions to the other side noticed.  They'll get a message that basically asks: 'Are you sure you want to be giving only to one side?  Don't you want to have friends on both sides of the aisle?'  If your interests are subject to anger from the other side of the aisle, you need to fear that you may suffer a penalty if you don't give...&nbsp;. [D]uring the 1990's, it became more and more acceptable to call someone, saying you saw he gave to this person, so he should also give to you or the person's opponent.'&nbsp;"  <em>Id.</em>, at 510 (Kollar-Kotelly, J.) (quoting Randlett Decl.   ;12, App. 715); 251 F. Supp. 2d, at 868 (Leon, J.) (same).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 48" name="FNopinion1.48" href="#FRopinion1.48">48</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;In addition to finding no support in our recent cases, see, <em>e.g.</em>, <em>Colorado II</em>, 453 U.&nbsp;S. 182 (1981), we upheld FECA's $5,000 limit on contributions to multicandidate political committees.  It is no answer to say that such limits were justified as a means of preventing individuals from using parties and political committees as pass-throughs to circumvent FECA's $1,000 limit on individual contributions to candidates.  Given FECA's definition of "contribution," the $5,000 and $25,000 limits restricted not only the source and amount of funds available to parties and political committees to make candidate contributions, but also the source and amount of funds available to engage in express advocacy and numerous other noncoordinated expenditures.  If indeed the First Amendment prohibited Congress from regulating contributions to fund the latter, the otherwise-easy-to-remedy exploitation of parties as pass-throughs (<em>e.g.</em>, a strict limit on donations that could be used to fund candidate contributions) would have provided insufficient justification for such overbroad legislation.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 49" name="FNopinion1.49" href="#FRopinion1.49">49</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;At another point, describing our "flawed reasoning," <em>Justice Kennedy </em>seems to suggest that Congress' interest in regulating the appearance of corruption extends only to those contributions that actually "create ... corrupt donor favoritism among ... officeholders." <em>Post</em>, at 16.  This latter formulation would render Congress' interest in stemming the appearance of corruption indistinguishable from its interest in preventing actual corruption.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 50" name="FNopinion1.50" href="#FRopinion1.50">50</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;In support of this claim, the political party plaintiffs assert that, in 2001, the RNC spent $15.6 million of nonfederal funds (30% of the nonfederal amount raised that year) on purely state and local election activity, including contributions to state and local candidates, transfers to state parties, and direct spending.  See Tr. of Oral Arg. 102-103 (statement of counsel Bobby R. Burchfield); 251 F. Supp. 2d, at 336-337 (Henderson, J.); <em>id</em>., at 464-465 (Kollar-Kotelly, J.); <em>id.</em>, at 830 (Leon, J.).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 51" name="FNopinion1.51" href="#FRopinion1.51">51</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The close relationship of federal officeholders and candidates to their parties answers not only <em>The</em> <em>Chief Justice</em>'s concerns about 323(a), but also his fear that our analysis of 323's remaining provisions bespeaks no limiting principle.  <em>Post</em>, at 6-7 (dissenting opinion).  As set forth in our discussion of those provisions, the record demonstrates close ties between federal officeholders and the state and local committees of their parties.  That close relationship makes state and local parties effective conduits for donors desiring to corrupt federal candidates and officeholders.  Thus, in upholding 323(b), (d), and (f), we rely not only on the fact that they regulate contributions used to fund activities influencing federal elections, but also that they regulate contributions to or at the behest of entities uniquely positioned to serve as conduits for corruption.  We agree with <em>The</em> <em>Chief Justice </em>that Congress could not regulate financial contributions to political talk show hosts or newspaper editors on the sole basis that their activities conferred a <em>benefit </em>on the candidate.  <em>Post</em>, at 7 (dissenting opinion).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 52" name="FNopinion1.52" href="#FRopinion1.52">52</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Plaintiffs claim that the option of soliciting hard money for state and local candidates is an illusory one, since several States prohibit state and local candidates from establishing multiple campaign accounts, which would preclude them from establishing separate accounts for federal funds.  See Cal. Fair Pol. Practs. Comm'n Advisory Op. A-91-448 (Dec. 16, 1991), 1991 WL 772902; Colo. Const., Art. XXVIII, 2(3); Iowa Code 56.5A (Supp. 2003); and Ohio Rev. Code Ann. 3517.10(J) (Anderson Supp. 2002).  Plaintiffs maintain that 323(a) combines with these state laws to make it impossible for state and local candidates to receive hard-money donations.  But the challenge we are considering is a facial one, and on its face 323(a) permits solicitations.  The fact that a handful of States might interfere with the mechanism Congress has chosen for such solicitations is an argument that may be addressed in an as-applied challenge.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 53" name="FNopinion1.53" href="#FRopinion1.53">53</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Even opponents of campaign finance reform acknowledged that "a prohibition of soft money donations to national party committees alone would be wholly ineffective."  The Constitution and Campaign Reform: Hearings on S. 522 before the Senate Committee on Rules and Administration, 106th Cong., 2d Sess., 301 (2000) (statement of Bobby R. Burchfield, Partner, Covington &amp; Burling).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 54" name="FNopinion1.54" href="#FRopinion1.54">54</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Generic campaign activity promotes a political party rather than a specific candidate.  2 U.&nbsp;S.&nbsp;C.&nbsp;A. 431(21).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 55" name="FNopinion1.55" href="#FRopinion1.55">55</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;A public communication is "a communication by means of any broadcast, cable, or satellite communication, newspaper, magazine, outdoor advertising facility, mass mailing, or telephone bank to the general public, or any other form of general public political advertising."  431(22).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 56" name="FNopinion1.56" href="#FRopinion1.56">56</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;So long as the communication does not constitute voter registration, voter identification, GOTV, or generic campaign activity.  431(20)(B)(i).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 57" name="FNopinion1.57" href="#FRopinion1.57">57</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Unless the contribution is earmarked for federal election activity.  431(20)(B)(ii).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 58" name="FNopinion1.58" href="#FRopinion1.58">58</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The statute gives the FEC responsibility for setting the allocation ratio.  441i(b)(2)(A); see also 11 CFR 300.33(b) (2003) (defining allocation ratios).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 59" name="FNopinion1.59" href="#FRopinion1.59">59</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;One former Senator noted:</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"&nbsp;'The fact is that much of what state and local parties do helps to elect federal candidates.  The national parties know it; the candidates know it; the state and local parties know it.  If state and local parties can use soft money for activities that affect federal elections, then the problem will not be solved at all. The same enormous incentives to raise the money will exist; the same large contributions by corporations, unions, and wealthy individuals will be made; the federal candidates who benefit from state party use of these funds will know exactly whom their benefactors are; the same degree of beholdenness and obligation will arise; the same distortions on the legislative process will occur; and the same public cynicism will erode the foundations of our democracy--except it will all be worse in the public's mind because a perceived reform was undercut once again by a loophole that allows big money into the system.'&nbsp;"  251 F.&nbsp;Supp. 2d, at 467 (Kollar-Kotelly, J.) (quoting Rudman Decl.   ;19, App. 746).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 60" name="FNopinion1.60" href="#FRopinion1.60">60</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;<em>E.g</em>.,<em> </em>251 F.&nbsp;Supp. 2d, at 479 (Kollar-Kotelly, J.) ("&nbsp;'It is ... not uncommon for the RNC to put interested donors in touch with various state parties.  This often occurs when a donor has reached his or her federal dollar limits to the RNC, but wishes to make additional contributions to the state party'&nbsp;" (quoting declaration of Thomas Josefiak, RNC Chief Counsel   ;68, App 308)); see also <em>Colorado II</em>, 533 U.&nbsp;S., at 458 (quoting Congressman Wayne Allard's Aug. 27, 1996, fundraising letter informing the recipient that "&nbsp;'you are at the limit of what you can directly contribute to my campaign,'&nbsp;" but "&nbsp;'you can further help my campaign by assisting the Colorado Republican Party'&nbsp;"); 251 F. Supp 2d,<em> </em>at 454 (Kollar-Kotelly, J.) ("&nbsp;'Both political parties have found spending soft money with its accompanying hard money match through their state parties to work smoothly, for the most part, and state officials readily acknowledge they are simply 'pass throughs' to the vendors providing the broadcast ads or direct mail'&nbsp;" (quoting Magleby Expert Report 37, App. 1510-1511.)).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 61" name="FNopinion1.61" href="#FRopinion1.61">61</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The 1998 Senate Report found that, in exchange for a substantial donation to <em>state</em> Democratic committees and candidates, the DNC arranged meetings for the donor with the President and other federal officials.  1 1998 Senate Report<em> </em>43-44; 2 <em>id.</em>, at 2907-2931; 5 <em>id</em>., at 7519.  That same Report also detailed how Native American tribes that operated casinos made sizable soft-money contributions to state Democratic committees in apparent exchange for access and influence.  1 <em>id</em>., at 44-46; 2 <em>id.,</em> at  3167-3194; see also McCain Decl., Exh. I (Weisskopf, The Busy Back-Door Men, Time, Mar. 31, 1997, p. 40)).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 62" name="FNopinion1.62" href="#FRopinion1.62">62</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Since voter identification is a necessary precondition of any GOTV program, the findings regarding GOTV funding obviously apply with equal force to the funding of voter identification efforts.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 63" name="FNopinion1.63" href="#FRopinion1.63">63</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;With respect to GOTV, voter identification, and other generic campaign activity, the FEC has interpreted 323(b) to apply only to those activities conducted after the earliest filing deadline for access to the federal election ballot or, in States that do not conduct primaries, after January 1 of even-numbered years.  11 CFR 100.24(a)(1) (2002).  Any activities conducted outside of those periods are completely exempt from regulation under 323(b).  Of course, this facial challenge does not present the question of the FEC regulations' constitutionality.  But the fact that the statute provides this basis for the FEC reasonably to narrow 301(20)(A)(ii) further calls into question plaintiffs' claims of facial overbreadth.  See<em> Broadrick</em> v. <em>Oklahoma, </em>413 U.&nbsp;S. 601, 613 (1973).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 64" name="FNopinion1.64" href="#FRopinion1.64">64</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;We likewise reject the argument that 301(20)(A)(iii) is unconstitutionally vague.  The words "promote," "oppose," "attack," and "support" clearly set forth the confines within which potential party speakers must act in order to avoid triggering the provision.  These words "provide explicit standards for those who apply them" and "give the person of ordinary intelligence a reasonable opportunity to know what is prohibited."  <em>Grayned</em> v. <em>City of Rockford,</em> 408 U.&nbsp;S. 104, 108-109 (1972).  This is particularly the case here, since actions taken by political parties are presumed to be in connection with election campaigns.  See <em>Buckley</em>, 413 U.&nbsp;S. 548, 580 (1973).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 65" name="FNopinion1.65" href="#FRopinion1.65">65</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Any doubts that donors would engage in such a seemingly complex scheme are put to rest by the record evidence in <em>Buckley</em> itself.  See n.&nbsp;6, <em>supra</em> (setting forth the Court of Appeals' findings regarding the efforts of milk producers to obtain a meeting with White House officials).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 66" name="FNopinion1.66" href="#FRopinion1.66">66</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Section 501(c) organizations are groups generally exempted from taxation under the Internal Revenue Code. 26 U.&nbsp;S.&nbsp;C. 501(a).  These include 501(c)(3) charitable and educational organizations, as well as 501(c)(4) social welfare groups.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 67" name="FNopinion1.67" href="#FRopinion1.67">67</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Section 527 "political organizations" are, unlike 501(c) groups, organized for the express purpose of engaging in partisan political activity.  They include any "party, committee, association, fund, or other organization (whether or not incorporated) organized and operated primarily for the purpose of directly or indirectly accepting contributions or making expenditures" for the purpose of "influencing or attempting to influence the selection, nomination, or appointment of any individual for Federal, State, or local public office."  26 U.&nbsp;S.&nbsp;C. 527(e).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 68" name="FNopinion1.68" href="#FRopinion1.68">68</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The record shows that many of the targeted tax-exempt organizations engage in sophisticated and effective electioneering activities for the purpose of influencing federal elections, including waging broadcast campaigns promoting or attacking particular candidates and conducting large-scale voter registration and GOTV drives.  For instance, during the final weeks of the 2000 presidential campaign, the NAACP's National Voter Fund registered more than 200,000 people, promoted a GOTV hotline, ran three newspaper print ads, and made several direct mailings.  251 F.&nbsp;Supp. 2d, at 348-349 (Henderson, J.).  The NAACP reports that the program turned out one million additional African-American voters and increased turnout over 1996 among targeted groups by 22% in New York, 50% in Florida, and 140% in Missouri.  <em>Ibid</em>.  The effort, which cost $10 million, was funded primarily by a $7 million contribution from an anonymous donor.  <em>Id.,</em> at 349 (citing cross-examination of Donald P. Green, Yale University 15-20, Exh. 3; see I Defs. Refiling Trs. on Pub. Record); 251 F.&nbsp;Supp. 2d, at 522 (Kollar-Kotelly, J.) (same); <em>id</em>., at 851 (Leon, J.) (same); see also <em>id.,</em> at 349 (Henderson, J.) (stating that in 2000 the National Abortion and Reproductive Rights Action League (NARAL) spent $7.5 million and mobilized 2.1 million pro-choice voters (citing declaration of Mary Jane Gallagher, Exec. V. P., NARAL, 8, App. 271-272,   ;24)); 251 F.&nbsp;Supp. 2d, at 522 (Kollar-Kotelly, J.) (same).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 69" name="FNopinion1.69" href="#FRopinion1.69">69</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Notably, the FEC has interpreted 323(d)(2) to permit state, district, and local party committees to solicit donations to 527 organizations that are state-registered PACs, that support only state or local candidates, and that do not make expenditures or disbursements in connection with federal elections.  11 CFR 300.37(a)(3)(iv) (2003).  The agency determined that this interpretation of "political committee"--at least with respect to state, district, and local committees--was consistent with BCRA's fundamental purpose of prohibiting soft money from being used in connection with federal elections.  67 Fed. Reg. 49106 (2002).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 70" name="FNopinion1.70" href="#FRopinion1.70">70</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Section 323(e)(1)(B) tightly constrains the ability of federal candidates and officeholders to solicit or spend nonfederal money in connection with state or local elections.  Contributions cannot exceed FECA's analogous hard-money contribution limits or come from prohibited sources.  In effect, 323(e)(1)(B) doubles the limits on what individuals can contribute to or at the behest of federal candidates and officeholders, while restricting the use of the additional funds to activities not related to federal elections.  If the federal candidate or officeholder is also a candidate for state or local office, he or she may solicit, receive, and spend an unlimited amount of nonfederal money in connection with that election, subject only to state regulation and the requirement that such solicitation or expenditures refer only to the relevant state or local office.  2 U.&nbsp;S.&nbsp;C.&nbsp;A. 441i(e)(2).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 71" name="FNopinion1.71" href="#FRopinion1.71">71</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See 148 Cong. Rec. S2143 (Mar. 20, 2002) (statement of Sen. Feingold) (Section 323(f) does not prohibit "spending non-federal money to run advertisements that mention that [state or local candidates] have been endorsed by a Federal candidate or say that they identify with a position of a named Federal candidate, so long as those advertisements do not support, attack, promote or oppose the Federal candidate").</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 72" name="FNopinion1.72" href="#FRopinion1.72">72</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;<em>Justice Kennedy</em> faults our "unwillingness" to confront that "Title I's entirety ... look[s] very much like an incumbency protection plan," citing 323(e), which provides officeholders and candidates with greater opportunities to solicit soft money than 323(a) and (d) permit party officers.  <em>Post</em>, at 23-24.  But, 323(e) applies to both officeholders <em>and candidates</em> and allows only <em>minimally </em>greater opportunities for solicitation out of regard for the fact that candidates and officeholders, unlike party officers, can never step out of their official roles.  <em>Supra</em>, at 70-71; 42 U.&nbsp;S.&nbsp;C.&nbsp;A. 441i(e).  Any concern that Congress might opportunistically pass campaign-finance regulation for self-serving ends is taken into account by the applicable level of scrutiny.  Congress must show concrete evidence that a particular type of financial transaction is corrupting or gives rise to the appearance of corruption and that the chosen means of regulation are closely drawn to address that real or apparent corruption.  It has done so here.  At bottom, <em>Justice Kennedy</em> has long disagreed with the basic holding of <em>Buckley</em> and its progeny that less rigorous scrutiny--which shows a measure of deference to Congress in an area where it enjoys particular expertise--applies to assess limits on campaign contributions.  <em>Colorado II</em>, 533 U.&nbsp;S., at 465 (<em>Thomas</em>, J., dissenting) (joining <em>Justice Thomas</em> for the proposition that "<em>Buckley</em> should be overrruled" (citation omitted)); <em>Shrink Missouri</em>, 528 U.&nbsp;S., at 405-410 (<em>Kennedy, J.</em>, dissenting).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 73" name="FNopinion1.73" href="#FRopinion1.73">73</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;BCRA also provides a "backup" definition of "electioneering communication," which would become effective if the primary definition were "held to be constitutionally insufficient by final judicial decision to support the regulation provided herein."  2 U.&nbsp;S.&nbsp;C.&nbsp;A. 434(f)(3)(A)(ii).  We uphold all applications of the primary definition and accordingly have no occasion to discuss the backup definition.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 74" name="FNopinion1.74" href="#FRopinion1.74">74</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;We then held that, so construed, the expenditure restriction did not advance a substantial government interest, because independent express advocacy did not pose a danger of real or apparent corruption, and the line between express advocacy and other electioneering activities was easily circumvented.  Concluding that 608(e)(1)'s heavy First Amendment burden was not justified, we invalidated the provision.  <em>Buckley,</em> 424 U.&nbsp;S., at 45-48.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 75" name="FNopinion1.75" href="#FRopinion1.75">75</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Our adoption of a narrowing construction was consistent with our vagueness and overbreadth doctrines.  See <em>Broadrick,</em> 413 U.&nbsp;S., at 613; <em>Grayned,</em> 408 U.&nbsp;S., at 108-114. </p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 76" name="FNopinion1.76" href="#FRopinion1.76">76</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The provision at issue in <em>MCFL</em>--2 U.&nbsp;S.&nbsp;C. 441b (1982 ed.)--required corporations and unions to use separate segregated funds, rather than general treasury moneys, on expenditures made "&nbsp;'in connection with'&nbsp;" a federal election.  <em>MCFL,</em> 479 U.&nbsp;S., at 241.  We noted that <em>Buckley</em> had limited the statutory term "&nbsp;'expenditure'&nbsp;" to words of express advocacy "in order to avoid problems of overbreadth."  479 U.&nbsp;S., at 248.  We held that "a similar <em>construction</em>" must apply to the expenditure limitation before us in <em>MCFL</em> and that the reach of 2 U.&nbsp;S.&nbsp;C. 441b was therefore constrained to express advocacy.  479 U.&nbsp;S., at 249 (emphasis added).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 77" name="FNopinion1.77" href="#FRopinion1.77">77</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;As one major-party political consultant testified, "&nbsp;'it is rarely advisable to use such clumsy words as "vote for" or "vote against."&nbsp;'&nbsp;"  251 F.&nbsp;Supp. 2d, at 305 (Henderson, J.) (quoting declaration of Douglas L. Bailey, founder, Bailey, Deardourff &amp; Assoc., 1-2, App. 24,   ;3).  He explained: "&nbsp;'All advertising professionals understand that the most effective advertising leads the viewer to his or her own conclusion without forcing it down their throat.'&nbsp;"  251 F.&nbsp;Supp. 2d, at 305 (Henderson, J.).  Other political professionals and academics confirm that the use of magic words has become an anachronism.  See <em>id</em>., at 531 (Kollar-Kotelly, J.) (quoting declaration of Raymond D. Strother, Pres., Strother/Duffy/Strother   ;4, 9 Defs. Exhs., Tab 40); see Unsealed Pp. Vol., Tab 7); App. 1334-1335 (Krasno &amp; Sorauf Expert Report)); see also 251 F.&nbsp;Supp. 2d, at 305 (Henderson, J.); <em>id.</em>, at 532 (Kollar-Kotelly, J.); <em>id.</em>, at 875-76 (Leon, J.).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 78" name="FNopinion1.78" href="#FRopinion1.78">78</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;One striking example is an ad that a group called "Citizens for Reform" sponsored during the 1996 Montana congressional race, in which Bill Yellowtail was a candidate.  The ad stated:</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"Who is Bill Yellowtail?  He preaches family values but took a swing at his wife.  And Yellowtail's response?  He only slapped her.  But 'her nose was not broken.'  He talks law and order ... but is himself a convicted felon.  And though he talks about protecting children, Yellowtail failed to make his own child support payments--then voted against child support enforcement.  Call Bill Yellowtail.  Tell him to support family values."  5 1998 Senate Report 6305 (minority views).</p>
<p>

The notion that this advertisement was designed purely to discuss the issue of family values strains credulity.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 79" name="FNopinion1.79" href="#FRopinion1.79">79</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;As discussed below, <em>infra</em>, at 97-103, BCRA 203 bars corporations and labor unions from funding electioneering communications with money from their general treasuries, instead requiring them to establish a "separate segregated fund" for such expenditures.  2 U.&nbsp;S.&nbsp;C.&nbsp;A. 441b(b)(2).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 80" name="FNopinion1.80" href="#FRopinion1.80">80</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Section 401 of BCRA provides:</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"If any provision of this Act or amendment made by this Act . . ., or the application of a provision or amendment to any person or circumstance, is held to be unconstitutional, the remainder of this Act and amendments made by this Act, and the application of the provisions and amendment to any person or circumstance, shall not be affected by the holding."  2 U.&nbsp;S.&nbsp;C.&nbsp;A. 454 note.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 81" name="FNopinion1.81" href="#FRopinion1.81">81</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The disclosure requirements that BCRA 201 added to FECA 304 are actually somewhat less intrusive than the comparable requirements that have long applied to persons making independent expenditures.  For example, the previous version of 304 required groups making independent expenditures to identify donors who contributed more than $200.  2 U. S. C. 434(c)(2)(C) (2000 ed.).  The comparable requirement in the amendments applies only to donors of $1,000 or more.  2 U.&nbsp;S.&nbsp;C.&nbsp;A. 434(f)(2)(E), (F) (Supp. 2003).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 82" name="FNopinion1.82" href="#FRopinion1.82">82</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;<em>NAACP</em> v. <em>Alabama </em>arose out of a judgment holding the NAACP<em> </em>in contempt for refusing to produce the names and addresses of its members and agents in Alabama.  The NAACP "made an uncontroverted showing that on past occasions revelation of the identity of its rank-and-file members ha[d] exposed these members to economic reprisal, loss of employment, threat of physical coercion, and other manifestations of public hostility."  357 U.&nbsp;S., at 462.  We thought it apparent that the compelled disclosure would "affect adversely" the NAACP and its members' ability "to pursue their collective effort to foster beliefs which they admittedly have the right to advocate."  <em>Id.</em>, at 462-463.  Under these circumstances, we concluded that Alabama's interest in determining whether the NAACP was doing business in the State was plainly insufficient to justify its production order.  <em>Id.</em>, at 464-466.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 83" name="FNopinion1.83" href="#FRopinion1.83">83</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;We stated:</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"The District Court properly applied the <em>Buckley</em> test to the facts of this case.  The District Court found 'substantial evidence of both governmental and private hostility toward and harassment of [Socialist Workers Party (SWP)] members and supporters.'  Appellees introduced proof of specific incidents of private and government hostility toward the SWP and its members within the four years preceding the trial.  These incidents, many of which occurred in Ohio and neighboring States, included threatening phone calls and hate mail, the burning of SWP literature, the destruction of SWP members' property, police harassment of a party candidate, and the firing of shots at an SWP office.  There was also evidence that in the 12-month period before trial 22 SWP members, including 4 in Ohio, were fired because of their party membership.  Although appellants contend that two of the Ohio firings were not politically motivated, the evidence amply supports the District Court's conclusion that 'private hostility and harassment toward SWP members make it difficult for them to maintain employment.'  The District Court also found a past history of Government harassment of the SWP."  <em>Brown</em> v. <em>Socialist Workers '74 Campaign Comm. (Ohio),</em> 459 U.&nbsp;S. 87, 98-99 (1982) (paragraph break omitted).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 84" name="FNopinion1.84" href="#FRopinion1.84">84</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;We cannot judge the likelihood that this will occur, as the record contains little if any description of the contractual provisions that commonly govern payments for electioneering communications.  Nor does the record contain any evidence relating to <em>Justice Kennedy</em>'s speculation, <em>post</em>, at 39-40, that advance disclosure may disadvantage an advertiser.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 85" name="FNopinion1.85" href="#FRopinion1.85">85</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;New FECA 315(a)(7)(C) reads as follows:</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"if --</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"(i) any person makes, or contracts to make, any disbursement for any electioneering communication (within the meaning of section 434(f)(3) of this title); and</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"(ii) such disbursement is coordinated with a candidate or an authorized committee of such candidate, a Federal, State, or local political party or committee thereof, or an agent or official of any such candidate, party, or committee;</p>
<p>

such disbursement or contracting shall be treated as a contribution to the candidate supported by the electioneering communication or that candidate's party and as an expenditure by that candidate or that candidate's party. . . ."  2 U.&nbsp;S.&nbsp;C.&nbsp;A. 441a(a)(7)(C).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 86" name="FNopinion1.86" href="#FRopinion1.86">86</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;We have explained:</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"The statutory purpose of 441b ... is to prohibit contributions or expenditures by corporations or labor organizations in connection with federal elections.  2 U.&nbsp;S.&nbsp;C. 441b(a).  The section, however, permits some participation of unions and corporations in the federal electoral process by allowing them to establish and pay the administrative expenses of 'separate segregated fund[s],' which may be 'utilized for political purposes.'  2 U.&nbsp;S.&nbsp;C. 441b(b)(2)(C).  The Act restricts the operations of such segregated funds, however, by making it unlawful for a corporation to solicit contributions to a fund established by it from persons other than its 'stockholders and their families and its executive or administrative personnel and their families.'  2 U.&nbsp;S.&nbsp;C. 441b(b)(4)(A)."  <em>National Right to Work,</em> 459 U.&nbsp;S., at 201-202.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 87" name="FNopinion1.87" href="#FRopinion1.87">87</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The amendment is straightforward.  Prior to BCRA, FECA 316(a) made it "unlawful ... for any corporation whatever, or any labor organization, to make a contribution or expenditure in connection with" certain federal elections.  2 U.&nbsp;S.&nbsp;C. 441b(a) (2000 ed.).  BCRA amends FECA 316(b)(2)'s definition of the term "contribution or expenditure" to include "any applicable electioneering communication."  2 U.&nbsp;S.&nbsp;C.&nbsp;A. 441b(b)(2) (Supp. 2003).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 88" name="FNopinion1.88" href="#FRopinion1.88">88</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;As <em>Justice Kennedy</em> emphasizes in dissent, <em>post</em>, at 44-45, we assume that the interests that justify the regulation of campaign speech might not apply to the regulation of genuine issue ads.  The premise that apparently underlies <em>Justice Kennedy</em>'s principal submission is a conclusion that the two categories of speech are nevertheless entitled to the same constitutional protection.  If that is correct, <em>Justice Kennedy</em> must take issue with the basic holding in <em>Buckley</em> and, indeed, with our recognition in <em>First Nat. Bank of Boston</em> v. <em>Bellotti,</em> 435 U.&nbsp;S. 765 (1978), that unusually important interests underlie the regulation of corporations' campaign-related speech.  In <em>Bellotti</em> we cited <em>Buckley</em>, among other cases, for the proposition that "[p]reserving the integrity of the electoral process, preventing corruption, and 'sustain[ing] the active, alert responsibility of the individual citizen in a democracy for the wise conduct of the government' are interests of the highest importance."  514 U.&nbsp;S. 334 (1995). </p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 89" name="FNopinion1.89" href="#FRopinion1.89">89</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;In a different but somewhat related argument, one set of plaintiffs contends that political campaigns and issue advocacy involve press activities, and that BCRA therefore interferes with speakers' rights under the Freedom of the Press Clause.   U.&nbsp;S. Const., Amdt. 1.  We affirm the District Court's conclusion that this contention lacks merit.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 90" name="FNopinion1.90" href="#FRopinion1.90">90</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The statutory scheme is somewhat complex.  In its provision dealing with "Rules Relating to Electioneering Communications," BCRA 203(c)(2) (adding FECA 316(c)(2)) makes a blanket exception for designated nonprofit organizations, which reads as follows:</p>
<p>

"Exception</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"Notwithstanding paragraph (1), the term 'applicable electioneering communication' does not include a communication by a section 501(c)(4) organization or a political organization (as defined in section 527(e)(1) of Title 26) made under section 434(f)(2)(E) or (F) of this title if the communication is paid for exclusively by funds provided directly by individuals who are United States citizens or nationals or lawfully admitted for permanent residence (as defined in section 1101(a)(20) of Title 8).  For purposes of the preceding sentence, the term 'provided directly by individuals' does not include funds the source of which is an entity described in subsection (a) of this section." 2 U.&nbsp;S.&nbsp;C.&nbsp;A. 441b(c)(2) (Supp. 2003).</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;BCRA 204, however, amends FECA 316(c) to exclude "targeted communications" from that exception.  New FECA 316(c)(6) states that the 316(c)(2) exception "shall not apply in the case of a targeted communication that is made by an organization described" in 316(b)(2).  2 U.&nbsp;S.&nbsp;C.&nbsp;A.   ;441b(c)(6)(A).  Subparagraph (B) then defines the term "targeted communication" for the purpose of the provision as including all electioneering communications.  The parties and the judges on the District Court have assumed that amended FECA 316(c)(6) completely canceled the exemption for nonprofit corporations set forth in 316(c)(2).  251 F.&nbsp;Supp. 2d, at 804 (Leon, J.) ("Section 204 completely cancels out the exemption for all nonprofit corporations provided by Section 203").</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 91" name="FNopinion1.91" href="#FRopinion1.91">91</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;"[A] unanimous Court in <em>National Right to Work</em> did not think the regulatory burdens on PACs, including restrictions on their ability to solicit funds, rendered a PAC unconstitutional as an advocacy corporation's sole avenue for making political contributions.  See 459 U.&nbsp;S., at 201-202. There is no reason to think the burden on advocacy corporations is any greater today, or to reach a different conclusion here." <em>Beaumont</em>, 539 U.&nbsp;S., at ___ (slip op., at 16).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 92" name="FNopinion1.92" href="#FRopinion1.92">92</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;New FECA 304(g) provides:</p>
<p>

"Time for reporting certain expenditures</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"(1) Expenditures aggregating $1,000</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"(A) Initial report</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"A person (including a political committee) that makes or contracts to make independent expenditures aggregating $1,000 or more after the 20th day, but more than 24 hours, before the date of an election shall file a report describing the expenditures within 24 hours.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"(B) Additional reports</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"After a person files a report under subparagraph (A), the person shall file an additional report within 24 hours after each time the person makes or contracts to make independent expenditures aggregating an additional $1,000 with respect to the same election as that to which the initial report relates."  2 U.&nbsp;S.&nbsp;C.&nbsp;A. 434 (Supp. 2003).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 93" name="FNopinion1.93" href="#FRopinion1.93">93</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;New FECA 315(d)(4) reads as follows:</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"Independent versus coordinated expenditures by party</p>
<p>

 &nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"(A) In general</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"On or after the date on which a political party nominates a candidate, no committee of the political party may make--</p>
<p>

 &nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"(i) any coordinated expenditure under this subsection with respect to the candidate during the election cycle at any time after it makes any independent expenditure (as defined in section 431(17) of this title) with respect to the candidate during the election cycle; or</p>
<p>

 &nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"(ii) any independent expenditure (as defined in section 431(17) of this title) with respect to the candidate during the election cycle at any time after it makes any coordinated expenditure under this subsection with respect to the candidate during the election cycle.</p>
<p>

 &nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"(B) Application</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"For purposes of this paragraph, all political committees established and maintained by a national political party (including all congressional campaign committees) and all political committees established and maintained by a State political party (including any subordinate committee of a State committee) shall be considered to be a single political committee.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"(C) Transfers</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"A committee of a political party that makes coordinated expenditures under this subsection with respect to a candidate shall not, during an election cycle, transfer any funds to, assign authority to make coordinated expenditures under this subsection to, or receive a transfer of funds from, a committee of the political party that has made or intends to make an independent expenditure with respect to the candidate."  2 U.&nbsp;S.&nbsp;C.&nbsp;A. 441a(d)(4) (Supp. 2003).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 94" name="FNopinion1.94" href="#FRopinion1.94">94</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;After exempting political parties from the general contribution and expenditure limitations of the statute, 2 U.&nbsp;S.&nbsp;C.&nbsp;A. 441a(d)(1), FECA 315(d) imposes the following substitute limitations on party spending:</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"(2) The national committee of a political party may not make any expenditure in connection with the general election campaign of any candidate for President of the United States who is affiliated with such party which exceeds an amount equal to 2 cents multiplied by the voting age population of the United States (as certified under subsection (e) of this section).  Any expenditure under this paragraph shall be in addition to any expenditure by a national committee of a political party serving as the principal campaign committee of a candidate for office of President of the United States.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"(3) The national committee of a political party, or a State committee of a political party, including any subordinate committee of a State committee, may not make any expenditure in connection with the general election campaign of a candidate for Federal office in a State who is affiliated with such party which exceeds--</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"(A) in the case of a candidate for election to the office of Senator, or of Representative from a State which is entitled to only one Representative, the greater of--</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"(i) 2 cents multiplied by the voting age population of the State (as certified under subsection (e) of this section); or</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"(ii) $20,000; and</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"(B) in the case of a candidate for election to the officer of Representative, Delegate, or Resident Commissioner in any other State, $10,000."  2 U.&nbsp;S.&nbsp;C. 441a(d)(2)-(3).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 95" name="FNopinion1.95" href="#FRopinion1.95">95</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;As amended by BCRA,&nbsp;301(17) provides:</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"Independent expenditure</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"The term 'independent expenditure' means an expenditure by a person--</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"(A) expressly advocating the election or defeat of a clearly identified candidate; and</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"(B) that is not made in concert or cooperation with or at the request or suggestion of such candidate, the candidate's authorized political committee, or their agents, or a political party committee or its agents."  2 U.&nbsp;S.&nbsp;C.&nbsp;A. 431(17) (Supp. 2003).</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The version of the definition prior to its amendment by BCRA also included the phrase "expressly advocating the election or defeat of a clearly identified candidate."  2 U.&nbsp;S.&nbsp;C. 431(17) (2000 ed.).  That definition had been adopted in 1976, presumably to reflect the narrowing construction that the Court adopted in <em>Buckley</em>.  Federal Election Campaign Act Amendments of 1976, 90 Stat. 475.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 96" name="FNopinion1.96" href="#FRopinion1.96">96</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Although the District Court and all the parties to this litigation endorse the interpretation set forth in the text, it is not clear that subparagraph (B) should be read so broadly:  The reference to "a State" instead of "the States" suggests that Congress meant to distinguish between committees associated with the party for each State (which would be grouped together by State, with each grouping treated as a single committee for purposes of the choice) and committees associated with a national party (which would likewise be grouped together and treated as a separate political committee).  We need not resolve the interpretive puzzle, however, because even under the more limited reading a local party committee would be able to tie the hands of a state committee or other local committees in the same State.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 97" name="FNopinion1.97" href="#FRopinion1.97">97</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The italicized portion of the following partial quotation of FECA 315(a)(7) was added by 214 of BCRA:</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"For purposes of this subsection--</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"(A) contributions to a named candidate made to any political committee authorized by such candidate to accept contributions on his behalf shall be considered to be contributions made to such candidate;</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"(B)(i) expenditures made by any person in cooperation, consultation, or concert, with, or at the request or suggestion of, a candidate, his authorized political committees, or their agents, shall be considered to be a contribution to such candidate;</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"(ii) <em>expenditures made by any person (other than a candidate or candidate's authorized committee) in cooperation, consultation, or concert with, or at the request or suggestion of, a national, State, or local committee of a political party, shall be considered to be contributions made to such party committee </em>...&nbsp;."  2 U.&nbsp;S.&nbsp;C.&nbsp;A. 441a(a)(7) (Supp. 2003).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 98" name="FNopinion1.98" href="#FRopinion1.98">98</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Pre-BCRA FEC regulations defined coordinated expenditures to include expenditures made "[a]t the request or suggestion of" a candidate or party; communications in which a candidate or party "exercised control or decision-making authority over the content, timing, location, mode, intended audience, volume of distribution, or frequency of placement"; and communications produced "[a]fter substantial discussion or negotiation" with a party or candidate, "the result of which is collaboration or agreement."  11 CFR 100.23(c)(2) (2001).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 99" name="FNopinion1.99" href="#FRopinion1.99">99</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Contrary to plaintiffs' contention, the statutory framework was not significantly different at the time of our decision in <em>Buckley</em>.  The relevant provision, 18 U.&nbsp;S.&nbsp;C. 608(e)(1), treated as coordinated any expenditures  "authorized <em>or requested</em> by the candidate."  (Emphasis added.)  And the legislative history, on which we relied for "guidance in differentiating individual expenditures that are contributions ... from those treated as independent expenditures," described as "independent" an expenditure made by a supporter "&nbsp;'completely on his own, and not at the request or suggestion of the candidate or his agen[t].'&nbsp;" 424 U.&nbsp;S., at 46-47, n. 53 (quoting S.&nbsp;Rep. No. 93-689, p.&nbsp;18 (1974)).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 1" name="FNopinion2.1" href="#FRopinion2.1">1</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;To qualify for increased candidate contribution limits, the "opposition personal funds amount," which depends on expenditures by a candidate and her self-financed opponent, must exceed a "threshold amount."  2 U.&nbsp;S.&nbsp;C.&nbsp;A. 441a(i)(1)(D), 441a-1(a)(2)(A) (Supp. 2003).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 2" name="FNopinion2.2" href="#FRopinion2.2">2</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;If the "opposition personal funds amount" is at least 10 times the "threshold amount" in a Senate race, or exceeds $350,000 in a House of Representatives race, the coordinated party expenditure limits do not apply.  441a(i)(1)(C)(iii), 441a-1(a)(1)(C).</p>

<br><h3>FOOTNOTES</h3><br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 3" href="#FRopinion3.*">*</a></strong><br><p>*<em>Justice Stevens, Justice O'Connor, Justice Souter</em>, and <em>Justice Ginsburg</em> join this opinion in its entirety.</p>

<br><h3>FOOTNOTES</h3><br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 1" href="#FRdissent1.*">*</a></strong><br><p>*&nbsp;J<em>ustice Scalia </em>and<em> Justice Kennedy </em>join this opinion in its<br>entirety.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 1" name="FNdissent1.1" href="#FRdissent1.1">1</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The Court points out that state parties may use Levin funds for certain activities.  Levin funds, however, are still federal restrictions on speech, even if they are less onerous than the restrictions placed on national parties.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 2" name="FNdissent1.2" href="#FRdissent1.2">2</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Ironically, in the Court's view, Congress cannot be trusted to exercise judgment independent of its parties' large donors in its usual voting decisions because donations may be used to further its members' reelection campaigns, but yet must be deferred to when it passes a comprehensive regulatory regime that restricts election-related speech.  It seems to me no less likely that Congress would create rules that favor its Members' reelection chances, than be corrupted by the influx of money to its political parties, which may in turn be used to fund a portion of the Members' reelection campaigns.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 3" name="FNdissent1.3" href="#FRdissent1.3">3</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The Court's suggestion that the "close relationship" between federal officeholders and state and local political parties in some way excludes the media from its rationale is unconvincing, see <em>ante,</em> at 24, n.&nbsp;15 (<em>Thomas</em>, J., concurring in part, concurring in result in part, and dissenting in part), particularly because such a relationship may be proved with minimal evidence.  Indeed, although the Court concludes that local political parties have a "close relationship" with federal candidates, thus warranting greater congressional regulation, I am unaware of <em>any</em> evidence in the record that indicates that local political parties have <em>any</em> relationship with federal candidates.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 4" name="FNdissent1.4" href="#FRdissent1.4">4</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;BCRA does not even close all of the "loopholes" that currently exist.  Nonprofit organizations are currently able to accept, without disclosing, unlimited donations for voter registration, voter identification, and get-out-the-vote activities, and the record indicates that such organizations already receive large donations, sometimes in the millions of dollars, for these activities, 251 F.&nbsp;Supp. 2d 176, 323 (DC 2003) (Henderson, J., concurring in judgment in part and dissenting in part) (noting that the NAACP Voter Fund received a single, anonymous $7 million donation for get-out-the-vote activities).  There is little reason why all donations to these nonprofit organizations, no matter the purpose for which the money is used, will deserve any more protection than the Court provides state parties if Congress decides to regulate them.  And who knows what the next "loophole" will be.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 5" name="FNdissent1.5" href="#FRdissent1.5">5</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Section 315(e), as amended by BCRA 504, provides:</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"Political record</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"(1) In general</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"A licensee shall maintain, and make available for public inspection, a complete record of a request to purchase broadcast time that--</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"(A) is made by or on behalf of a legally qualified candidate for public office; or</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"(B) communicates a message relating to any political matter of national importance, including--</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"(i) a legally qualified candidate;</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"(ii) any election to Federal office; or</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"(iii) a national legislative issue of public importance.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"(2) Contents of record</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"A record maintained under paragraph (1) shall contain information regarding--</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"(A) whether the request to purchase broadcast time is accepted or rejected by the licensee;</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"(B) the rate charged for the broadcast time;</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"(C) the date and time on which the communication is aired;</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"(D) the class of time that is purchased;</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"(E) the name of the candidate to which the communication refers and the office to which the candidate is seeking election, the election to which the communication refers, or the issue to which the communication refers (as applicable);</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"(F) in the case of a request made by, or on behalf of, a candidate, the name of the candidate, the authorized committee of the candidate, and the treasurer of such committee; and</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"(G) in the case of any other request, the name of the person purchasing the time, the name, and phone number of a contact person for such person, and a list of the chief executive officers or members of the executive committee or of the board of directors of such person.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"(3) Time to maintain file</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"The information required under this subsection shall be placed in a political file as soon as possible and shall be retained by the licensee for a period of not less than 2 years."</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 6" name="FNdissent1.6" href="#FRdissent1.6">6</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Communications relating to candidates will be covered by the new Communications Act 315(e)(1)(A), so, in this context, we must consider, for example, the plaintiff-organizations, which may attempt<br>to use the broadcast medium to convey a message espoused by the organizations.</p>

Hearings before the Senate Committee on Armed Services, 83d Cong., 1st Sess., 26 (1953).</p>

<br><h3>FOOTNOTES</h3><br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 2" href="#FRother2.*">*</a></strong><br><p>*&nbsp;&nbsp;<em>Justice Scalia</em> joins Parts I, II-A, and II-B of this opinion.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 1" name="FNother2.1" href="#FRother2.1">1</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The Court also rejected an overbreadth challenge, reasoning that "Congress was justified in concluding that the interest in safeguarding against the appearance of impropriety requires that the opportunity for abuse inherent in the process of raising large monetary contributions be eliminated."  <em>Buckley</em>, 424 U.&nbsp;S., at 30.  But this justification was inextricably intertwined with the Court's concern over the difficulty of isolating suspect contributions.  If it were easy to isolate suspect contributions, and if bribery laws could be quickly and effectively enforced, then there would be no "opportunity for abuse inherent in the process," <em>ibid., </em>and hence no need for an otherwise overbroad contribution ceiling.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 2" name="FNother2.2" href="#FRother2.2">2</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Arguably, the current antibribery statute, 18 U.&nbsp;S.&nbsp;C. 201, is broad enough to cover the unspecified other "attempts ... to influence governmental action" that the <em>Buckley</em> Court seemed worried about.  424 U.&nbsp;S., at 28.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 3" name="FNother2.3" href="#FRother2.3">3</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Indeed, the principal contents of Senator McCain's declaration are his complaints that several bills he supported were defeated. The Senator also suggests, without evidence, that there had been some connection between the defeat of his favored policy outcomes and certain soft-money donors.  See, <em>e.g.</em>, App. 393-394,   ;10 (declaration of Sen. John McCain   ;10) (noting Democratic "parliamentary procedural device" used to block one of Senator McCain's proposed amendments to the Sarbanes-Oxley corporate governance bill).  The possibility that his favored policy outcomes lost due to lack of public support, or because the opponents of the amendment honestly believed it would do harm to the public, does not appear to be addressed.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 4" name="FNother2.4" href="#FRother2.4">4</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Former Senators Simpson and Simon both seem to have the same response as Senator McCain, see n.&nbsp;3, <em>supra, </em>in having their favored interests voted down, and similarly do not consider alternative explanations for the failure of their proposals.  See App. 811,   ;11 (declaration of former Sen. Alan Simpson   ;11); App. 805,   ;14 (declaration of former Sen. Paul Simon   ;14).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 5" name="FNother2.5" href="#FRother2.5">5</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Because there is not an iota of evidence supporting the Government's asserted interests in BCRA 318, I concur in the Court's conclusion that this provision is unconstitutional.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 6" name="FNother2.6" href="#FRother2.6">6</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The joint opinion continues yet another disturbing trend: the application of a complaisant level of scrutiny under the guise of "strict scrutiny."  See <em>Grutter</em> v. <em>Bollinger</em>, 539 U.&nbsp;S. ___ (2003).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 7" name="FNother2.7" href="#FRother2.7">7</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;This is doubly so now that the Court has decided that there is no constitutional need for the showing even of an "agreement" in order to transform an expenditure into a "coordinated expenditur[e]" and hence into a contribution for FECA purposes.  <em>Ante</em>, at 115-117 (joint opinion).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 8" name="FNother2.8" href="#FRother2.8">8</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The National Rifle Association (NRA) plaintiffs compellingly state that "[a]s a measure designed to prevent official corruption, of either the <em>quid pro quo</em> or the 'gratitude' variety, Title II ... makes no more sense than a bribery statute requiring corporations to pay for their bribes using funds from PACs."  Brief for Appellants NRA et&nbsp;al. in No. 02-1675, pp.&nbsp;24-25.  And, regarding the appearance of corruption: "Defendants' own witnesses concede that the public's perceptions of ads is not affected in the slightest by whether they are purchased with general treasury funds or with PAC money."  <em>Id</em>., at 25.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 9" name="FNother2.9" href="#FRother2.9">9</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The fact that the Founders located the right to anonymous speech in the "freedom of the press" is of no moment, as "it makes little difference in terms of our analysis, which seeks to determine only whether the First Amendment, as originally understood, protects anonymous writing."  <em>McIntyre</em>, 514 U.&nbsp;S., at 360 (<em>Thomas</em>, J., concurring).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 10" name="FNother2.10" href="#FRother2.10">10</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;BCRA 212(a) is also unconstitutional.  Although the plaintiffs only challenge the advance disclosure requirement of 212(a), by requiring disclosure of communications using express advocacy, the entire reporting requirement is unconstitutional for the same reasons that 201 is unconstitutional.  Consequently, it follows that the advance disclosure provision is unconstitutional.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;BCRA 311 and 504 also violate the First Amendment.  By requiring any television or radio advertisement that satisfies the definition of "electioneering communication" to include the identity of the sponsor, and even a "full-screen view of a representative of the political committee or other person making the statement" in the case of a television advertisement, new FECA 318, 311 is a virtual carbon copy of the law at issue in <em>McIntyre</em> v. <em>Ohio Elections Comm'n, </em>514 U.&nbsp;S. 334 (1995) (the only difference being the irrelevant distinction between a printed pamphlet and a television or radio advertisement).  And 504 not only has the precise flaws of 201, but also sweeps broadly as well, covering any "message relating to any political matter of national importance, including ... a national legislative issue of public importance."  Hence, both 311 and 504 should be struck down.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 11" name="FNother2.11" href="#FRother2.11">11</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The Court, in upholding most of its provisions by concluding that the "express advocacy" limitation derived by <em>Buckley</em> is not a constitutionally mandated line, has, in one blow, overturned every Court of Appeals that has addressed this question (except, perhaps, one).  See <em>Clifton</em> v. <em>FEC</em>, 114 F.&nbsp;3d 1309, 1312 (CA1 1997); <em>Vermont Right to Life Comm., Inc.</em> v. <em>Sorrell</em>, 221 F.&nbsp;3d 376, 387 (CA2 2000); <em>FEC</em> v. <em>Christian Action Network, Inc</em>., 110 F.&nbsp;3d 1049, 1064 (CA4 1997); <em>Chamber of Commerce</em> v. <em>Moore</em>, 288 F.&nbsp;3d 187, 193 (CA5 2000); <em>Iowa Right to Life Comm., Inc.</em> v. <em>Williams</em>, 187 F.&nbsp;3d 963, 968-970 (CA8 1999); <em>Citizens for Responsible Govt. State Political Action Comm.</em> v. <em>Davidson</em>, 236 F.&nbsp;3d 1174, 1187 (CA10 2000).  The one possible exception is the Ninth Circuit.  See <em>FEC</em> v. <em>Furgatch</em>, 807 F.&nbsp;2d 857, 862-863 (1987).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 12" name="FNother2.12" href="#FRother2.12">12</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;After all, the constitutional avoidance doctrine counsels us to adopt constructions of statutes to "avoid decision of constitutional questions," not to deliberately create constitutional questions.  <em>United States</em> v. <em>Thirty-seven Photographs,</em> 402 U.&nbsp;S. 363, 373 (1971); see also <em>United States ex rel. Attorney General</em> v. <em>Delaware &amp; Hudson Co.,</em> 213 U.&nbsp;S. 366, 408 (1909).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 13" name="FNother2.13" href="#FRother2.13">13</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;This very case is an excellent example of why such a bright-line rule is necessary.  The Court, having "rejected the notion that the First Amendment requires Congress to treat so-called issue advocacy differently from express advocacy," <em>ante</em>, at 87-88, proceeds to uphold significant new restrictions on speech that is, in every sense of the word, pure issue-related speech.  The Court abandons the bright-line rule, and now subjects political speech of virtually any kind to the risk of regulation by Congress.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 14" name="FNother2.14" href="#FRother2.14">14</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Chief Justice Burger presciently commented on precisely this point in <em>First Nat. Bank of Boston </em>v. <em>Bellotti,</em> 435 U.&nbsp;S. 765, 796-797 (1978) (citations omitted):</p>
<p>

"In terms of 'unfair advantage in the political process' and 'corporate domination of the electoral process,' it could be argued that such media conglomerates as I describe pose a much more realistic threat to valid interests than do appellants and similar entities not regularly concerned with shaping popular opinion on public issues.  See <em>Miami Herald Publishing Co.</em> v. <em>Tornillo</em>, [418 U.&nbsp;S. 241 (1974)].  In <em>Tornillo</em>, for example, we noted the serious contentions advanced that a result of the growth of modern media empires 'has been to place in a few hands the power to inform the American people and shape public opinion.'  418 U.&nbsp;S., at 250."</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 15" name="FNother2.15" href="#FRother2.15">15</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;It appears that "circumvention" of the campaign finance laws by exploiting media exemptions is already being planned by one of the plaintiffs in this litigation.  See Theimer, <em>NRA Seeks Status as News Outlet</em>, Washington Post A09 (Dec. 7, 2003) (reporting that the NRA is looking to acquire a broadcast outlet and seeking to be classified as a news organization).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 16" name="FNother2.16" href="#FRother2.16">16</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Given the quality of the evidence the Court relies upon to uphold Title I, the evidence should not be hard to come by.  See Kane &amp; Preston, Fox Chief on Hot Seat, Roll Call (June 12, 2003) ("GOP leaders such as House Majority Leader Tom DeLay (R-Texas) have labeled CNN as the 'Communist News Network' and the 'Clinton News Network'--suggesting they only presented the liberal viewpoint and that of former President Clinton"); Jones, Fox News Moves from the Margins to the Mainstream, Shorenstein Center, Harvard (Dec. 1, 2002) (quoting Al Gore as describing Fox News and the Washington Times as "part and parcel of the Republican Party").</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 17" name="FNother2.17" href="#FRother2.17">17</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See also 4 W. Blackstone, Commentaries on the Laws of England 151 (1769) ("The liberty of the press is indeed essential to the nature of a free state").</p>

<br><h3>FOOTNOTES</h3><br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 3" href="#FRother3.*">*</a></strong><br><p>*&nbsp;<em>The Chief Justice</em> joins this opinion in its entirety.  <em>Justice Scalia</em> joins this opinion except to the extent it upholds new FECA 323(e)<br>and BCRA 202.  <em>Justice Thomas</em> joins this opinion with respect to BCRA 213.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 3" name="FNother3." href="#FRother3."></a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See also <em>Federal Election Commission</em> v. <em>Beaumont</em>, 539 U.&nbsp;S. ___, ___ (2003) (slip op., at 14) ("[T]he basic premise we have followed in setting First Amendment standards for reviewing political financial restrictions [is that] the level of scrutiny is based on the importance of the 'political activity at issue' to effective speech or political association"); <em>California Democratic Party</em> v. <em>Jones</em>, 530 U.&nbsp;S. 567, 582 (2000) ("We can think of no heavier burden on a political party's associational freedom.  Proposition 198 is therefore unconstitutional unless it is narrowly tailored to serve a compelling state interest").</p>
 
        <p></p>
        <p></p>

    <div class="case-information">
        <h2 class="case-information__title"> McCONNELL v. FEDERAL ELECTION COMM'N [02-1674], 540 U.S. 93 (2003)</h2>
        <p class="case-information__paragraph"><b class="case-information__paragraph-topic">Citation:</b> 540 U.S. 93</p>
        <p class="case-information__paragraph"><b class="case-information__paragraph-topic">Docket No:</b> No. 02-1674</p>
        
        
        <p class="case-information__paragraph"><b class="case-information__paragraph-topic">Argued:</b> September 08, 2003</p>
        <p class="case-information__paragraph"><b class="case-information__paragraph-topic">Decided:</b> December 10, 2003</p>
        <p class="case-information__paragraph"><b class="case-information__paragraph-topic">Court:</b> United States Supreme Court</p>

<div class="cardLinks list section"><div class="fl-card">
    <div class="fl-card__header">

</main><script>_satellite["_runScript1"](function(event, target, Promise) {
_satellite.ads = {};

_satellite.ads.adInfoFactory = function(nameString, href) {
  // TODO remove this once the correct PA is being used for the lid
  if( _satellite.getVar('ad_decision_context') && _satellite.getVar('ad_decision_context').pa ) {
    nameString = nameString.replace(/pa:[0-9]+/, "pa:"+_satellite.getVar('ad_decision_context').pa);  
  }
  _satellite.logger.debug('_satellite.ads.adInfoFactory nameString: '+nameString);

  // sample name: "&lid=clk:fs/prod:dts/cid:5088127/msa:259/pa:1313/sub:3107462/pos:1"
  var s = nameString;
  s = s.substr(5);
  var lid = s;
  s = s.replaceAll(':','":"');
  s = s.replaceAll('/','","');
  s = '{"'+s+'"}'
  var adInfo = JSON.parse(s);
  adInfo.lid = lid;
  adInfo.paid = (typeof adInfo.sub != 'undefined');
  adInfo.adId = (adInfo.paid ? adInfo.sub : adInfo.cid);
  var map = {
    'ph':'phone',
    'fs':'website',
    'pv':'profile',
    'cu':'contact',
    'vr':'review-read',
    'wr':'review-write',
    'sl':'super-lawyers'
  };  
  adInfo.type = map[adInfo.clk];   
  adInfo.transactionId = _satellite.getVar('fl_user_id')+'-'+adInfo.adId+'-'+(new Date().getTime());
  
  if( href && adInfo.type == 'phone' ) {
    adInfo.detail = href.replace(/[^\d]/g,'');
    if( adInfo.detail.length > 10 ) {
      adInfo.detail = adInfo.detail.substr(adInfo.detail.length-10);
    }
  }  
  
  return adInfo;
}

_satellite.ads.trackAdEngagement = function(adInfo) {
  if( typeof adInfo.adId == 'undefined' ) {
    return false;
  }
  if( typeof FLDataLayer.ads == 'undefined' ) {
    FLDataLayer.ads = {};
  }
  if( typeof FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements == 'undefined' ) {
    FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements = [];
  }
  for( var i=0; i < FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements.length; i++ ) {
    if( FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements[i].type == adInfo.type && FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements[i].adId == adInfo.adId ) {
      return true;
    }
  }

  FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements.push(adInfo);
  return false;
}

_satellite.ads.sendConversionFindLaw = function() {
  var subId = _satellite.getVar('current_ad_eng').sub;
  var cookieName = 'fl_ad_'+subId;
  var cookieValue = 'true';
  var c = _satellite.cookie.get(cookieName);
  // we use a cookie to ensure multiple beacons are not sent for the same ad
  if( typeof c == 'undefined' ) {
    _satellite.setFlCookie(cookieName, cookieValue, 1);

    var data = {};
    var clickUrlElement = document.getElementById(_satellite.getVar('ad_eng_sub'));
    if (clickUrlElement !== null) {
      data.clickUrl = clickUrlElement.innerText;
    }
    if( _satellite.getVar('ad_decision_context') ) {
      data.adDecisionContext = _satellite.getVar('ad_decision_context');
    }
    if( _satellite.getVar('ad_eng_transaction_id') ) {
      data.transactionId = _satellite.getVar('ad_eng_transaction_id');
    }
    if( _satellite.getVar('ad_eng_sub') ) {
      data.subId = _satellite.getVar('ad_eng_sub');
    }
    if( _satellite.getVar('fl_user_id') ) {
      data.flUserId = _satellite.getVar('fl_user_id');
    }
    if( _satellite.getVar('fl_tag_track') == 'false' ) {
      data.internalFlag = 'true'; // mimick as an internal request
    }

    let url = new URL(window.location.href);
    let domain = 'attorneys.findlaw'+(_satellite.getVar('cms_env') == 'prod' ? '' : '-stage')+'.com';
    let attorneysUrl = new URL('https://'+domain+'/flstats/v1/stats');
    let searchParams = new URLSearchParams(window.location.search);

    if (searchParams.has('ads-server-env')) {
      attorneysUrl.searchParams.set('ads-server-env', url.searchParams.get('ads-server-env'));
    }
    navigator.sendBeacon(attorneysUrl.href, JSON.stringify(data));
  }
}

    expireDays = dt.toGMTString();
  }
  document.cookie = cookieName+"="+cookieValue+"; expires="+expireDays+'; path='+path+'; domain='+_satellite.getVar('host_root');
};

        const pageType = _satellite.getVar("page_page_type");
        if(pageType == "article") toStore += " Article";

      _satellite.writeCrossSubdomainCookie("s_dsource", toStore);
    };

    _satellite.slowDown = function(e, goTo){
      e.preventDefault(); // prevent default anchor behavior
      setTimeout(function() {
        window.location = goTo;
      }, 300);
    }
    
    //CTA Search - enter & click
    const nextStepsForm = document.querySelector('.nextSteps form');
    _satellite.logger.info('nextStepsForm',nextStepsForm);
    if (nextStepsForm) {
          nextStepsForm.addEventListener('submit', function(e) {
          _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Next Steps");

      component.addEventListener('keypress', function(e) {
        if (e.key === 'Enter') {
          if (location.host + location.pathname == "lawyers.findlaw.com/") {
            _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Find a Lawyer");
            _satellite.logger.info('Search Box','Find a Lawyer');
          } else {
              _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Top-Nav");
              _satellite.logger.info('Search Box','Top-Nav');
          }
        }
      });
    })
    

    //Top Nav & Find a Lawyer Page - click

      _satellite.logger.info('button',button);
      button.addEventListener('click', function(e) {
        if (location.host + location.pathname === "lawyers.findlaw.com/") {
          _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Find a Lawyer");
          _satellite.logger.info('Search Box','Find a Lawyer');
        } else {
          _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Top-Nav");
          _satellite.logger.info('Search Box','Top-Nav');
        }
      });
    })

    //Homepage search
    if (location.host + location.pathname === "www.findlaw.com/") {

      _satellite.logger.info('forms',forms);
      forms.forEach((form) => {
        _satellite.logger.info('form',form);
        form.addEventListener('submit', function() {
          _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Home");
          _satellite.logger.info('Search Box','Home');
        });
      });
    }

          _satellite.trackSearchSource(source);
